</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239400231" /><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Internet_protocol_suite" title="Template:Internet protocol suite"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Internet_protocol_suite" title="Template talk:Internet protocol suite"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Internet_protocol_suite" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Internet protocol suite"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Hypertext Transfer Protocol Secure</b> (<b>HTTPS</b>) is an extension of the <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">Hypertext Transfer Protocol</a> (HTTP). It uses <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encryption</a> for <a href="/wiki/Secure_communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Secure communications">secure communication</a> over a <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">computer network</a>, and is widely used on the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In HTTPS, the <a href="/wiki/Communication_protocol" title="Communication protocol">communication protocol</a> is encrypted using <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a> (TLS) or, formerly, <a href="/wiki/Secure_Sockets_Layer" class="mw-redirect" title="Secure Sockets Layer">Secure Sockets Layer</a> (SSL). The protocol is therefore also referred to as <b>HTTP over TLS</b>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or <b>HTTP over SSL</b>.
</p><p>The principal motivations for HTTPS are <a href="/wiki/Authentication" title="Authentication">authentication</a> of the accessed <a href="/wiki/Website" title="Website">website</a> and protection of the <a href="/wiki/Information_privacy" title="Information privacy">privacy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Data_integrity" title="Data integrity">integrity</a> of the exchanged data while it is in transit. It protects against <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attacks</a>, and the bidirectional <a href="/wiki/Block_cipher_mode_of_operation" title="Block cipher mode of operation">block cipher encryption</a> of communications between a <a href="/wiki/Client_(computing)" title="Client (computing)">client</a> and <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">server</a> protects the communications against <a href="/wiki/Eavesdropping" title="Eavesdropping">eavesdropping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tamper-evident#Tampering" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamper-evident">tampering</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-httpse&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-httpse-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The authentication aspect of HTTPS requires a trusted third party to sign server-side <a href="/wiki/Public_key_certificate" title="Public key certificate">digital certificates</a>. This was historically an expensive operation, which meant fully authenticated HTTPS connections were usually found only on secured payment transaction services and other secured corporate information systems on the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a>. In 2016, a campaign by the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a> with the support of <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a> developers led to the protocol becoming more prevalent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> HTTPS has since 2018<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> been used more often by web users than non-secure HTTP, primarily to protect page authenticity on all types of websites, secure accounts, and keep user communications, identity, and web browsing private.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Overview">Overview</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Internet2.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Internet2.svg/250px-Internet2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="201" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Internet2.svg/500px-Internet2.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1288" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URL</a> beginning with the HTTPS scheme and the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">WWW</a> domain name label</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Uniform_Resource_Identifier" title="Uniform Resource Identifier">Uniform Resource Identifier</a> (URI) scheme <i>HTTPS</i> has identical usage syntax to the HTTP scheme. However, HTTPS signals the browser to use an added encryption layer of SSL/TLS to protect the traffic. SSL/TLS is especially suited for HTTP, since it can provide some protection even if only one side of the communication is <a href="/wiki/Authentication" title="Authentication">authenticated</a>. This is the case with HTTP transactions over the Internet, where typically only the <a href="/wiki/Web_server" title="Web server">server</a> is authenticated (by the client examining the server's <a href="/wiki/Public_key_certificate" title="Public key certificate">certificate</a>).
</p><p>HTTPS creates a secure channel over an insecure network. This ensures reasonable protection from <a href="/wiki/Eavesdropping" title="Eavesdropping">eavesdroppers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attacks</a>, provided that adequate <a href="/wiki/Cipher_suite" title="Cipher suite">cipher suites</a> are used and that the server certificate is verified and trusted.
</p><p>Because HTTPS piggybacks HTTP entirely on top of TLS, the entirety of the underlying HTTP protocol can be encrypted. This includes the request's <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URL</a>, query parameters, headers, and cookies (which often contain identifying information about the user). However, because website addresses and <a href="/wiki/Port_(computer_networking)" title="Port (computer networking)">port</a> numbers are necessarily part of the underlying <a href="/wiki/TCP/IP" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP/IP">TCP/IP</a> protocols, HTTPS cannot protect their disclosure. In practice this means that even on a correctly configured web server, eavesdroppers can infer the IP address and port number of the web server, and sometimes even the domain name (e.g. www.example.org, but not the rest of the URL) that a user is communicating with, along with the amount of data transferred and the duration of the communication, though not the content of the communication.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-httpse&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-httpse-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Web browsers know how to trust HTTPS websites based on <a href="/wiki/Certificate_authority" title="Certificate authority">certificate authorities</a> that come pre-installed in their software. Certificate authorities are in this way being trusted by web browser creators to provide valid certificates. Therefore, a user should trust an HTTPS connection to a website <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> all of the following are true:
</p>
<ul><li>The user trusts that their device, hosting the browser and the method to get the browser itself, is not compromised (i.e. there is no <a href="/wiki/Supply_chain_attack" title="Supply chain attack">supply chain attack</a>).</li>
<li>The user trusts that the browser software correctly implements HTTPS with correctly pre-installed certificate authorities.</li>
<li>The user trusts the certificate authority to vouch only for legitimate websites (i.e. the certificate authority is not compromised and there is no mis-issuance of certificates).</li>
<li>The website provides a valid certificate, which means it was signed by a trusted authority.</li>
<li>The certificate correctly identifies the website (e.g., when the browser visits "<a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://example.com">https://example.com</a>", the received certificate is properly for "example.com" and not some other entity).</li>
<li>The user trusts that the protocol's encryption layer (SSL/TLS) is sufficiently secure against eavesdroppers.</li></ul>
<p>HTTPS is especially important over insecure networks and networks that may be subject to tampering.  Insecure networks, such as public <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> access points, allow anyone on the same local network to <a href="/wiki/Packet_analyzer" title="Packet analyzer">packet-sniff</a> and discover sensitive information not protected by HTTPS. Additionally, some free-to-use and paid <a href="/wiki/Wireless_LAN" title="Wireless LAN">WLAN</a> networks have been observed tampering with webpages by engaging in <a href="/wiki/Packet_injection" title="Packet injection">packet injection</a> in order to serve their own ads on other websites. This practice can be exploited maliciously in many ways, such as by injecting <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a> onto webpages and stealing users' private information.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>HTTPS is also important for connections over the <a href="/wiki/Tor_(network)" title="Tor (network)">Tor network</a>, as malicious Tor nodes could otherwise damage or alter the contents passing through them in an insecure fashion and inject malware into the connection. This is one reason why the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Tor_Project" title="The Tor Project">the Tor Project</a> started the development of <a href="/wiki/HTTPS_Everywhere" title="HTTPS Everywhere">HTTPS Everywhere</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-httpse&#95;4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-httpse-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which is included in Tor Browser.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As more information is revealed about global <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance" title="Mass surveillance">mass surveillance</a> and criminals stealing personal information, the use of HTTPS security on all websites is becoming increasingly important regardless of the type of Internet connection being used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Even though <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> about individual pages that a user visits might not be considered sensitive, when aggregated it can reveal a lot about the user and compromise the user's privacy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-deployhttpscorrectly&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deployhttpscorrectly-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Deploying HTTPS also allows the use of <a href="/wiki/HTTP/2" title="HTTP/2">HTTP/2</a> and <a href="/wiki/HTTP/3" title="HTTP/3">HTTP/3</a> (and their predecessors <a href="/wiki/SPDY" title="SPDY">SPDY</a> and <a href="/wiki/QUIC" title="QUIC">QUIC</a>), which are new HTTP versions designed to reduce page load times, size, and latency.
</p><p>It is recommended to use <a href="/wiki/HTTP_Strict_Transport_Security" title="HTTP Strict Transport Security">HTTP Strict Transport Security</a> (HSTS) with HTTPS to protect users from man-in-the-middle attacks, especially <a href="/wiki/Moxie_Marlinspike#SSL_stripping" title="Moxie Marlinspike">SSL stripping</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-deployhttpscorrectly&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deployhttpscorrectly-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>HTTPS should not be confused with the seldom-used <a href="/wiki/Secure_Hypertext_Transfer_Protocol" title="Secure Hypertext Transfer Protocol">Secure HTTP</a> (S-HTTP) specified in RFC 2660.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Usage_in_websites">Usage in websites</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Usage in websites"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As of April&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 33.2% of Alexa top 1,000,000 websites use HTTPS as default<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and 70% of page loads (measured by Firefox Telemetry) use HTTPS.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of June&#160;2025<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 71.2% of the Internet's  150,000 most popular websites have a secure implementation of HTTPS (up from 58.4% in December 2022),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, despite <a href="/wiki/TLS_1.3" class="mw-redirect" title="TLS 1.3">TLS 1.3</a>'s release in 2018, adoption has been slow, with many still remaining on the older TLS 1.2 protocol.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Browser_integration">Browser integration</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Browser integration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">browsers</a> display a warning if they receive an invalid certificate. Older browsers, when connecting to a site with an invalid certificate, would present the user with a <a href="/wiki/Dialog_box" title="Dialog box">dialog box</a> asking whether they wanted to continue. Newer browsers display a warning across the entire window. Newer browsers also prominently display the site's security information in the <a href="/wiki/Address_bar" title="Address bar">address bar</a>. <a href="/wiki/Extended_validation_certificate" class="mw-redirect" title="Extended validation certificate">Extended validation certificates</a> show the legal entity on the certificate information. Most browsers also display a warning to the user when visiting a site that contains a mixture of encrypted and unencrypted content. Additionally, many <a href="/wiki/Content-control_software" class="mw-redirect" title="Content-control software">web filters</a> return a security warning when visiting prohibited websites.
</p>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a>, opining that "In an ideal world, every web request could be defaulted to HTTPS", has provided an add-on called HTTPS Everywhere for <a href="/wiki/Mozilla_Firefox" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozilla Firefox">Mozilla Firefox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium_(web_browser)" title="Chromium (web browser)">Chromium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a>, which enables HTTPS by default for hundreds of frequently used websites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Forcing a web browser to load only HTTPS content has been supported in Firefox starting in version 83.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Starting in version 94, Google Chrome is able to "always use secure connections" if toggled in the browser's settings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Security">Security</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Security"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security#Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security §&#160;Security</a></div>
<p>The security of HTTPS is that of the underlying TLS, which typically uses long-term <a href="/wiki/Public-key_cryptography" title="Public-key cryptography">public</a> and private keys to generate a short-term <a href="/wiki/Session_key" title="Session key">session key</a>, which is then used to encrypt the data flow between the client and the server. <a href="/wiki/X.509" title="X.509">X.509</a> certificates are used to authenticate the server (and sometimes the client as well). As a consequence, <a href="/wiki/Certificate_authority" title="Certificate authority">certificate authorities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_key_certificate" title="Public key certificate">public key certificates</a> are necessary to verify the relation between the certificate and its owner, as well as to generate, sign, and administer the validity of certificates. While this can be more beneficial than verifying the identities via a <a href="/wiki/Web_of_trust" title="Web of trust">web of trust</a>, the <a href="/wiki/2013_mass_surveillance_disclosures" class="mw-redirect" title="2013 mass surveillance disclosures">2013 mass surveillance disclosures</a> drew attention to certificate authorities as a potential weak point allowing <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attacks</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An important property in this context is <a href="/wiki/Forward_secrecy" title="Forward secrecy">forward secrecy</a>, which ensures that encrypted communications recorded in the past cannot be retrieved and decrypted should long-term secret keys or passwords be compromised in the future. Not all web servers provide forward secrecy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ecdhe&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecdhe-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Does this still hold in 2015? (February 2015)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>For HTTPS to be effective, a site must be completely hosted over HTTPS.  If some of the site's contents are loaded over HTTP (scripts or images, for example), or if only a certain page that contains sensitive information, such as a log-in page, is loaded over HTTPS while the rest of the site is loaded over plain HTTP, the user will be vulnerable to attacks and surveillance. Additionally, <a href="/wiki/HTTP_cookie" title="HTTP cookie">cookies</a> on a site served through HTTPS must have the <a href="/wiki/Secure_cookie" title="Secure cookie">secure attribute</a> enabled.  On a site that has sensitive information on it, the user and the session will get exposed every time that site is accessed with HTTP instead of HTTPS.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-deployhttpscorrectly&#95;14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deployhttpscorrectly-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Technical">Technical</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Technical"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Difference_from_HTTP">Difference from HTTP</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Difference from HTTP"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>HTTPS <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URLs</a> begin with "https://" and use <a href="/wiki/List_of_TCP_and_UDP_port_numbers" title="List of TCP and UDP port numbers">port</a> 443 by default, whereas <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a> URLs begin with "http://" and use port 80 by default.
</p><p>HTTP is not encrypted and thus is vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle" class="mw-redirect" title="Man-in-the-middle">man-in-the-middle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eavesdropping_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Eavesdropping attack">eavesdropping attacks</a>, which can let attackers gain access to website accounts and sensitive information, and modify webpages to inject <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a> or advertisements. HTTPS is designed to withstand such attacks and is considered secure against them (with the exception of HTTPS implementations that use deprecated versions of SSL).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Network_layers">Network layers</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Network layers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>HTTP operates at the highest layer of the <a href="/wiki/TCP/IP_model" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP/IP model">TCP/IP model</a>—the <a href="/wiki/Application_layer" title="Application layer">application layer</a>; as does the <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">TLS</a> security protocol (operating as a lower sublayer of the same layer), which encrypts an HTTP message prior to transmission and decrypts a message upon arrival. Strictly speaking, HTTPS is not a separate protocol, but refers to the use of ordinary <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a> over an <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encrypted</a> SSL/TLS connection.
</p><p>HTTPS encrypts all message contents, including the HTTP headers and the request/response data. With the exception of the possible <a href="/wiki/Chosen-ciphertext_attack" title="Chosen-ciphertext attack">CCA</a> cryptographic attack described in the <a href="#Limitations">limitations</a> section below, an attacker should at most be able to discover that a connection is taking place between two parties, along with their domain names and IP addresses.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Server_setup">Server setup</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Server setup"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>To prepare a web server to accept HTTPS connections, the administrator must create a <a href="/wiki/Public_key_certificate" title="Public key certificate">public key certificate</a> for the web server. This certificate must be signed by a trusted <a href="/wiki/Certificate_authority" title="Certificate authority">certificate authority</a> for the web browser to accept it without warning. The authority certifies that the certificate holder is the operator of the web server that presents it. Web browsers are generally distributed with a list of <a href="/wiki/Root_certificate" title="Root certificate">signing certificates of major certificate authorities</a> so that they can verify certificates signed by them.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Acquiring_certificates">Acquiring certificates</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Acquiring certificates"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A number of commercial <a href="/wiki/Certificate_authority" title="Certificate authority">certificate authorities</a> exist, offering paid-for SSL/TLS certificates of a number of types, including <a href="/wiki/Extended_Validation_Certificate" title="Extended Validation Certificate">Extended Validation Certificates</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Encrypt" title="Let&#39;s Encrypt">Let's Encrypt</a>, launched in April 2016,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-softpedia-launch&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-softpedia-launch-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> provides free and automated service that delivers basic SSL/TLS certificates to websites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a>, Let's Encrypt will make switching from HTTP to HTTPS "as easy as issuing one command, or clicking one button."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The majority of web hosts and cloud providers now leverage Let's Encrypt, providing free certificates to their customers.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Use_as_access_control">Use as access control</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Use as access control"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The system can also be used for client <a href="/wiki/Authentication" title="Authentication">authentication</a> in order to limit access to a web server to authorized users. To do this, the site administrator typically creates a certificate for each user, which the user loads into their browser. Normally, the certificate contains the name and e-mail address of the authorized user and is automatically checked by the server on each connection to verify the user's identity, potentially without even requiring a password.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In_case_of_compromised_secret_(private)_key"><span id="In_case_of_compromised_secret_.28private.29_key"></span>In case of compromised secret (private) key</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In case of compromised secret (private) key"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An important property in this context is <a href="/wiki/Forward_secrecy" title="Forward secrecy">perfect forward secrecy</a> (PFS). Possessing one of the long-term asymmetric secret keys used to establish an HTTPS session should not make it easier to derive the short-term session key to then decrypt the conversation, even at a later time. <a href="/wiki/Diffie%E2%80%93Hellman_key_exchange" title="Diffie–Hellman key exchange">Diffie–Hellman key exchange</a> (DHE) and <a href="/wiki/Elliptic-curve_Diffie%E2%80%93Hellman" title="Elliptic-curve Diffie–Hellman">Elliptic-curve Diffie–Hellman</a> key exchange (ECDHE) are in 2013 the only schemes known to have that property. In 2013, only 30% of Firefox, Opera, and Chromium Browser sessions used it, and nearly 0% of Apple's <a href="/wiki/Safari_(web_browser)" title="Safari (web browser)">Safari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer" title="Internet Explorer">Microsoft Internet Explorer</a> sessions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ecdhe&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecdhe-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> TLS 1.3, published in August 2018, dropped support for ciphers without forward secrecy. As of February&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 96.6% of web servers surveyed support some form of forward secrecy, and 52.1% will use forward secrecy with most browsers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of July&#160;2023<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 99.6% of web servers surveyed support some form of forward secrecy, and 75.2% will use forward secrecy with most browsers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Certificate_revocation">Certificate revocation</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Certificate revocation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Certificate_revocation" title="Certificate revocation">Certificate revocation</a></div>
<p>A certificate may be revoked before it expires, for example because the secrecy of the private key has been compromised. Newer versions of popular browsers such as <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Opera_(web_browser)" title="Opera (web browser)">Opera</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer" title="Internet Explorer">Internet Explorer</a> on <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> implement the <a href="/wiki/Online_Certificate_Status_Protocol" title="Online Certificate Status Protocol">Online Certificate Status Protocol</a> (OCSP) to verify that this is not the case. The browser sends the certificate's serial number to the certificate authority or its delegate via OCSP (Online Certificate Status Protocol) and the authority responds, telling the browser whether the certificate is still valid or not.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The CA may also issue a <a href="/wiki/Certificate_revocation_list" title="Certificate revocation list">CRL</a> to tell people that these certificates are revoked. CRLs are no longer required by the CA/Browser forum,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="CRLs are actually required. (April 2025)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> nevertheless, they are still widely used by the CAs. Most revocation statuses on the Internet disappear soon after the expiration of the certificates.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RS&#95;1&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS_1-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Limitations">Limitations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Limitations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>SSL (Secure Sockets Layer) and TLS (Transport Layer Security) encryption can be configured in two modes: <i>simple</i> and <i>mutual</i>. In simple mode, authentication is only performed by the server. The mutual version requires the user to install a personal <a href="/wiki/Client_certificate" title="Client certificate">client certificate</a> in the web browser for user authentication.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In either case, the level of protection depends on the correctness of the <a href="/wiki/Implementation" title="Implementation">implementation</a> of the software and the <a href="/wiki/Cipher" title="Cipher">cryptographic algorithms</a> in use.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>SSL/TLS does not prevent the indexing of the site by a <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web crawler</a>, and in some cases the <a href="/wiki/Uniform_resource_identifier" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform resource identifier">URI</a> of the encrypted resource can be inferred by knowing only the intercepted request/response size.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This allows an attacker to have access to the <a href="/wiki/Plaintext" title="Plaintext">plaintext</a> (the publicly available static content), and the <a href="/wiki/Ciphertext" title="Ciphertext">encrypted text</a> (the encrypted version of the static content), permitting a <a href="/wiki/Chosen-ciphertext_attack" title="Chosen-ciphertext attack">cryptographic attack</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Because <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">TLS</a> operates at a protocol level below that of HTTP and has no knowledge of the higher-level protocols, TLS servers can only strictly present one certificate for a particular address and port combination.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the past, this meant that it was not feasible to use <a href="/wiki/Virtual_hosting#Name-based" title="Virtual hosting">name-based virtual hosting</a> with HTTPS. A solution called <a href="/wiki/Server_Name_Indication" title="Server Name Indication">Server Name Indication</a> (SNI) exists, which sends the hostname to the server before encrypting the connection, although older browsers do not support this extension. Support for SNI is available since <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a> 2, <a href="/wiki/Opera_(web_browser)" title="Opera (web browser)">Opera</a> 8, <a href="/wiki/Safari_(web_browser)" title="Safari (web browser)">Apple Safari</a> 2.1, <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> 6, and <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_7" title="Internet Explorer 7">Internet Explorer 7</a> on <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A sophisticated type of <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attack</a> called SSL stripping was presented at the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Black_Hat_Briefings" title="Black Hat Briefings">Blackhat Conference</a>. This type of attack defeats the security provided by HTTPS by changing the <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">https:</code> link into an <code class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-text mw-content-ltr" style="" dir="ltr">http:</code> link, taking advantage of the fact that few Internet users actually type "https" into their browser interface: they get to a secure site by clicking on a link, and thus are fooled into thinking that they are using HTTPS when in fact they are using HTTP. The attacker then communicates in clear with the client.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This prompted the development of a countermeasure in HTTP called <a href="/wiki/HTTP_Strict_Transport_Security" title="HTTP Strict Transport Security">HTTP Strict Transport Security</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>HTTPS has been shown to be vulnerable to a range of <a href="/wiki/Traffic_analysis" title="Traffic analysis">traffic analysis</a> attacks. Traffic analysis attacks are a type of <a href="/wiki/Side-channel_attack" title="Side-channel attack">side-channel attack</a> that relies on variations in the timing and size of traffic in order to infer properties about the encrypted traffic itself. Traffic analysis is possible because SSL/TLS encryption changes the contents of traffic, but has minimal impact on the size and timing of traffic. In May 2010, a research paper by researchers from <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Research" title="Microsoft Research">Microsoft Research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indiana_University_Bloomington" title="Indiana University Bloomington">Indiana University</a> discovered that detailed sensitive user data can be inferred from side channels such as packet sizes. The researchers found that, despite HTTPS protection in several high-profile, top-of-the-line web applications in healthcare, taxation, investment, and web search, an eavesdropper could infer the illnesses/medications/surgeries of the user, his/her family income, and investment secrets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The fact that most modern websites, including Google, Yahoo!, and Amazon, use HTTPS causes problems for many users trying to access public Wi-Fi hot spots, because a <a href="/wiki/Captive_portal" title="Captive portal">captive portal</a> Wi-Fi hot spot login page fails to load if the user tries to open an HTTPS resource.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several websites, such as <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://nossl.sh">NoSSL.sh</a>, guarantee that they will always remain accessible by HTTP<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=HTTPS&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security#History_and_development" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security §&#160;History and development</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Netscape_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Netscape Communications">Netscape Communications</a> created HTTPS in 1994 for its <a href="/wiki/Netscape_Navigator" title="Netscape Navigator">Netscape Navigator</a> web browser.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Originally, HTTPS was used with the <a href="/wiki/Secure_Sockets_Layer" class="mw-redirect" title="Secure Sockets Layer">SSL</a> protocol.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The original SSL protocol was developed by <a href="/wiki/Taher_Elgamal" title="Taher Elgamal">Taher Elgamal</a>, chief scientist at <a href="/wiki/Netscape" title="Netscape">Netscape Communications</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Messmer&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Messmer-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Greene&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greene-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Oppliger&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppliger-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As SSL evolved into <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a> (TLS), HTTPS was formally specified by RFC 2818<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in May 2000. Google announced in February 2018 that its Chrome browser would mark HTTP sites as "Not Secure" after July 2018.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This move was to encourage website owners to implement HTTPS, as an effort to make the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a> more secure.
</p>
